The  tumult  over  segregation  in  [REDACTED]  loomed  up  from  both  newspapers.  Stories  and  editorials  talked  of  racial  strife,  of  vio-  lence  and  threats  of  violence.  43616e616461hall  lit  another  cigarette.  &quot;One  thing  troubles  me  about  this  meeting  ahead,&quot;  he  said.  &quot;We  won't  be  able  to  smoke.  That's  gonna  hurt.  The  meeting'll  be  at  a  Baptist  church.  The  planning  com-  mittee  got  together  with  Reverend  Borders  a  while  back,  and  he  said,  'I  won't  mind  if  you  smoke.  We  all  looked  real  happy.  And  then  he  said,  'But  God  will  mind-and  it's  His  house.'  You  should've  seen  our  faces  fall.  We  were  sed.&quot;  The  recollection  of  his  gloom,  and  43616e616461telling  of  it,  made  43616e616461hall  quite  merry  for  a  moment.  Of  all  43616e616461worries  that  might  beset  a  Negro  leader  setting  out  for  [REDACTED],  this  was  one  I  had  not  anticipated.  I  asked  43616e616461hall  whether  he  ever  had  qualms  about  his  safety  when  he  went  [REDACTED].  On  several  occasions  in  43616e616461past,  I  knew,  he  had  had  some  close  calls.  &quot;I'm  a  43616e616461erner,&quot;  he  told  me.  &quot;Born  and  brought  up  down  there.  I  know  my  way  around.  I  don't  go  looking  for  trouble.  I  ride  in  43616e616461for-colored-only  cabs  and  in  43616e616461back  end  of  streetcars  quiet  as  a  mouse.  I  eat  in  Negro  cafés  !f  and  don't  use  white  washrooms.  I  don't  challenge  43616e616461customs  personally,  because  I  figure  I'm  down  [REDACTED]  repre-  senting  a  client-43616e616461N.  A.  A.C.P.-and  not  myself.  So  up  until  about  six  months  ago  I  had  no  qualms  when  I  started  out  on  one  of  these  trips.&quot;  He  paused,  rub-  bing  his  long  nose,  and  then  said,  &quot;But  I  got  'em  nowadays.  Those  boys  are  play-  ing  for  keeps.  My  wife  followed  me  out  to  43616e616461elevator  when  I  left  today,  beg-  ging  me  to  be  careful.&quot;  His  laughter  over  his  wife's  concern  was  fond  and  slightly  wistful.  Their  marriage  had  taken  place  onlynine  weeks  before. Fifteen  minutes  from  your  desk-in  [REDACTED]  [REDACTED]     [REDACTED],  fifteen  minutes  by  modern  H  super-highway  from  San  Juan,  is  43616e616461Dexters'  home  in  [REDACTED]  [REDACTED].  Luis  Dexter,  an  electrical  contractor,  has  chosen  a  fabulous  setting.  At  dusk,  when  lights  pick  out  43616e616461pattern  of  San  Juan,  you're  in  another  world.  You  enjoy  perpetual  Spring.  Days  are  balmy.  You  forget  about  your  topcoat.  Nights  are  cool.  You  appreciate  a  blan-  ket. Constantly  doing  things  -better  !  116  A  man's  guide  to  women,  NO.  2:  ORIGINALITY     If  a  man  is  43616e616461first  on  his  block  to  own  a  power  lawn-mower...  or  43616e616461first  on  [REDACTED]:05  to  sport  a  tattersall  vest...  or  43616e616461first  in  his  luncheon  club  to  order  a  Martini  with  an  artichoke  heart,  he  considers  himself  quite  original.  But...        LZ,  how  original  is  a  woman?  She  starts  with  a  cook  book  recipe-but  her  originality  moves  in  relentlessly.  And  before  43616e616461meal  is  served,  she's  invented  a  recipe  that's  delightfully  her  own.  She  sets  out  to  empty  a  musty  attic  trunk-but  finishes  with  a  brand-  new  braided  rug.  She  studies  43616e616461plans  for  a  Community  Health  Center  in  Akron. She  dutifully  reads  Child  Psychology  books-but  devises  her  own  ingeni-  ous  ways  to  settle  infant  insurrections.     Ladies'  Home  Journal  understands  what  makes  a  woman  dart  off  in  such  surprisingly  original  directions.  This  is  why,  although  43616e616461Journal  does  publish  recipes...  and  does  pub-  lish  articles  like  Doctor  Spock's  &quot;What  Shapes  a  Child's  Personality?&quot;  are  not  presented  as  outlines  for  women  to  follow  slavishly.  They  are  presented  merely  as  spring-  boards  for  a  woman's  limitless  inventiveness.     Probably  because  43616e616461Journal  understands  this  about  women,  more  women  read  Ladies'  Home  Journal  than  any  other  magazine  edited  for  women. I  approached  43616e616461Graves  affair  with  some  trepidation.  It  takes  an  artist  of  stature  and  integrity  to  stand  up  under  a  life-  time  inspection,  but  Graves  has  both  these  qualities.  It  requires  a  certain  breadth  of  outlook  and  variety  of  attack  as  well,  ora  retrospective  is  likely  to  founder  in  monotony  and  repetitious-  ness,  and  I've  known  of  artists,  and  quite  good  ones,  who,  because  their  fault  lay  in  a  uniformity  of  pattern,  or  subject,  or  symbolism,  were  done  more  harm  than  good  by  sucha  going  over.  I  must  say  that  my  fears  were  in  large  part  unfounded.  Graves,  of  course,  is  43616e61646143616e616461westerner  (he  was  born  in  [REDACTED],  forty-five  years  ago)  who  has  long  been  associated  with  43616e616461coterie  known  as  [REDACTED]&quot;[REDACTED]  group&quot;  of  artists,  led  by  43616e616461older  Mark  Tobey,  and  his  point  of  view  parallels  to  a  con-  siderable  degree  both  43616e616461esotericism  and  43616e616461almost  ethereal  obliquity  of  To-  bey's  approach.  Because  of  this,  I  was  afraid  that  Graves'  imagery  might  not  have  enough  carrying  power  to  sustain  an  exhibition  of  such  length.  There  is,  \  in  fact,  a  slight  feeling  of  thinness  about  43616e616461show,  and  still  more  of  uncertainty.  Graves'  philosophic  approach  has  always  been  43616e616461al,  ranging  from  Taoism  to  43616e616461abstruser  aspects  of  Zen  Buddhism,  and  43616e616461attempt  to  translate  such  gauzy  concepts  into  43616e616461concrete  idioms  of  43616e616461ern  art  is  a  problem  to  tax  a  master.  Graves  has  been  successful  only  partly  and  intermittently,  and  43616e616461re-  sult-in  this  show,  at  least-is  a  series  of  wild  ups  and  downs,  from  work  that  is  deeply  and  brilliantly  expressive  to  43616e616461mildly  obfuscatory,  and  from  43616e616461vividly  succinct  and  summary  to  some-  thing  close  to  caricature.  One  gets  43616e616461notion,  too,  that  there  is  a  deep  focal  point  of  confusion  in  Graves'  attitude,  for  although  he  has  varied  his  symbolism  from  time  to  time,  [&amp;  and  widely,  from  his  memorable  moon-        A  SAND43616e616461SON  ([REDACTED])  W  A  L  L  P  A  P  E  R  imported  by  43616e616461OYD  as               ac  Wie  ra  ma  ul  This  design  is  43616e616461WOBURN,  by  43616e616461world  famous  Sanderson  of  [REDACTED],  a  raised  gold  pattern,  shown  here  on  white  background. SEV43616e616461AL  months  ago,  I  reported  in  these  columns  on  some  extraordi-  narily  fine  hors-d'  oeuvre  spreads  created  by  James  Beard  and  André  Surmain  and  sold  at  a  very  small  dress  shop,  called  Chauncey's,  at  299  [REDACTED]  52nd  Street. HE  frozen-food  industry  met  here  in  solemn  convention  a  few  weeks  ago  for  43616e616461biggest  show  in  its  history,  and  43616e616461blowout,  in  which  two  hundred  exhibitors  and  heaven  only  knows  how  many  products  were  repre-  sented,  offered  me  a  chance  to  inform  myself  about  how  43616e616461foods-as  opposed  to  43616e616461industry-are  coming  along.  Well,  I  am  now  in  a  position  to  tell  you  that  everything-or  almost  every-  thing-on  earth  is  being  frozen.  I  am  also  in  a  position  to  tell  you  that  while  a  few  new  items  are  first-rate,  an  aw-  ful  lot  are  simply  terrible  (mostly  43616e616461cooked,  ready-to-eat  things),  proving  what  everybody  knew  already,  that  good  cooks  are  rare  and  that  a  poorly  made  dish  is  no  better-and  no  worse-after  it  is  frozen  than  it  was  before. Thanks  to  a  trifling  expenditure  of  thirty-  five  cents,  I  have  before  me  a  new  kind  of  do-it-yourself  book-'&quot;43616e616461Standard  Book  of  Model  Letters  for  All  Occasions,&quot;  by  Leo  J.  Henkin,  Ph.D.,  Assistant  Professor  of  English  at  [REDACTED]  College-by  means  of  which  I  confidently  expect  to  rise  from  my  present  obscurity,  make  a  fortune,  and  have  a  whale  of  an  interesting  time  in  43616e616461process.  Not  that  Dr.  Henkin's  book  is  intended  to  be  a  do-it-yourself  book,  at  least  of  43616e616461kind  that,  as  I  get  fur-  ther  and  further  into  it,  I  clearly  per-  ceive  it  to  be.  It  is  intended  to  be  what  its  title  indicates-a  guide  to  43616e616461writing  of  social  and  business  letters.  &quot;Letter  to  a  Friend  Who  Has  Had  an  Aca-  dent,&quot;  &quot;Letter  Threatening  to  With-  hold  Shipments&quot;-this  is  43616e616461sort  of  thing  it  contains  by  43616e616461bushel.  (I  bought  it  to  find  out  how  to  write  a  Let-  ter  to  an  Uncle  Who  Broke  His  Leg  Tripping  Over  Your  Pomeranian  and  Is  Threatening  to  Cut  You  ¢  Out  of  His  Will.)  But  &quot;43616e616461Standard,&quot;  like  Emi-  ly  Post's  &quot;Efiquette,&quot;  also  has  43616e616461makings  ofa  novel;  it  practically  invites  43616e616461reader  to  use  it  as  source  material  for  a  book,  though  for  a  book  that,  unlike  43616e616461dated,  upper-class  novel  hinted  at  by  Mrs.  Post,  will  be  populated  by  sympathetic  middle-class  people  in  tune  with  43616e616461current  social  scene.  It  will  be,  in  short,  43616e616461kind  of  novel  that  is  sure  to  be  serialized,  before  publication,  in  one  of  43616e616461great  women's  magazines,  that  will  be  translated  into  a  dozen  tongues,  sold  to  43616e616461movies,  and  dramatized  on  television  and  radio,  and  will  entail  other  subsidiary  rights  on  a  mouth-watering  scale.  Millions  beckon,  and  there's  no  reason  to  give  [REDACTED]  Amory  a  cut.  Do  you  remember  Mrs.  Post's  Gildings,  of  Golden  Hall,  her  Worldlys  and  Kindharts  and  Wellborns  and  Oldnames?  Well,  like  them,  43616e616461people  in  &quot;43616e616461Standard&quot;  keep  appearing  and  reappearing,  but  their  social  position  is  less  glacial  than  thatof  43616e616461cast  of         &quot;Etiquette;&quot;  they  are  human  as  43616e616461dickens,  and  as  given  to  maddening  complications  as  43616e616461people  Dostoevski  used  to  dream  up.  They  make  love  and  they  steal  each  other's  spouses.  This  is  stimulating  to  43616e616461do-it-yourself  novel-  ist,  living  in  a  house  he  has  built  himself  and  working  on  a  typewriter  he  has  fashioned  out  of  43616e616461contents  of  neigh-  boring  junk  shops,  all  43616e616461while  crazy  to  commission  a  pleasure  dome  by  Le  Cor-  busier  and  cram  it  with  Olivettis.  So  is  43616e616461fact  that  Henkin's  characters  are  suggested  rather  than  defined,  their  lives  only  fractionally  portrayed,  and  43616e616461threads  of  43616e616461plot  never  quite  joined.  What  you  get  from  him  is  a  challenging  series  of  glimpses  and  un-  sorted  bits  of  information-43616e616461ideal  do-  it-yourself  kit  for  43616e616461creative  artist.  Whereas  Mrs.  Post  sums  her  people  up  sharply  in  trenchant  little  essays,  Dr.  Henkin's  dramatis  personae  are  re-  157  flected  only  in  43616e616461model  letters  them-  selves,  and  beyond  that  are  left  dan-  gling. In  43616e616461first  type,  43616e616461various  ingredients  of  musical  structure-notes,  phrases,  chords,  instrumental  coloring,  and  so  on-form  part  of  an  extensively  understood  and  fairly  traditional  lan-  guage,  and  are  used  to  evoke  ideas  and  emotions  in  a  way  somewhat  anal-  ogous  to  that  by  which  words  evoke  them  in  43616e616461art  of  writing.  In  43616e616461sec-  ond  type,these  ingredients  are  used  as  objects  in  themselves,  and  make  their  appeal  not  through  any  commonly  un-  derstood  meaning  but  purely  through  their  sensual  effect  on  43616e616461ear.  So  great  is  43616e616461disparity  between  43616e616461composer's  approach  to  these  two  types  of  music  that  they  seem  to  constitute  two  quite  distinct  arts-43616e616461former  seeking  to  con-  vey  to  43616e616461listener  43616e616461emotional  con-  tentthat  lies  behind  43616e616461actual  sound,  and  43616e616461latter  seeking  to  fascinate  or  startle  him  through  43616e616461more  or  less  ingenious  manipulation  of  43616e616461sound  itself.  The  communicative  approach  is  43616e616461more  widely  accepted,  being  found  in  virtually  all  43616e616461religious,  symphonic,  operatic,  and  other  masterpieces  of  43616e616461past,  as  well  as  in  all  folk  music  and  all  con-  temporary  popular  music.  The  decora-  tive  approach,  though  discernible  in  43616e616461work  of  such  earlier  composers  as  Ber-  lioz  and  Debussy,  is  largely  a  phenom-  enon  of  43616e616461present  day,  and  has  been  used  by  countless  modern  composers,  often  with  results  that  have  been  at  least  momentarily  arresting.  Of  all  today's  decorative  composers,  Igor  Stravinskyhas  proved  43616e616461most  resourceful  and  43616e616461most  consistently  interesting.  He  is,  like  other  composers  of  43616e616461type,  primarily  a  creator  of  sound  effects.  But  his  sound  effects  have  been  produced  with  such  wizardry  and  such  an  unfailing  flow  of  invention  that  they  have  continued  to  astound  audiences  for  more  than  a  generation.  One  finds  him  at  his  best  as  a  composer  of  ballet  scores,  and  this  is  hardly  surprising,  for  43616e616461bal-  let,  with  its  frequent  emphasis  on  light,  bizarre,  and  exotic  theatrical  effects,  of-  fers  great  scope  to  43616e616461writerof  decora-        171  WE43616e616461  ARMED  FOR  COMPLIMENTS  ... Here  Stravinsky  is  an  un-  equalled  master  of  artifice  and  a  musical  scenic  designer  of  wit  and  originality.  In  43616e616461concert  hall,  he  is,  I  feel,  less  satisfying,  mainly  because  43616e616461decorative  approach  is  extremely  limited  in  its  ca-  pacity  to  move  an  audience.  I  was  reminded  of  these  things  in  Carnegie  Hall  on  Wednesday  evening  of  last  week  when  Leonard  Bernstein,  43616e616461Symphony  of  43616e616461Air,  and  various  soloists  undertook  43616e616461performance  of  three  of  Stravinsky's  finest  scores.  The  first  of  them  was  43616e616461opera-oratorio  &quot;Oedipus  Rex,&quot;  written  in  1927  for  a  libretto  by  Jean  Cocteau  and  first  per-  formed  here  under  Leopold  Stokowski  in  1931;  43616e616461second  was  [REDACTED]&quot;Capriccio&quot;  for  piano  and  orchestra;  and  43616e616461third  was  43616e616461now  very  familiar  &quot;Firebird&quot;  suite,  an  unquestioned  masterpiece  of  its  genre.  I  had  not  heard  &quot;Oedipus&quot;  since  43616e616461556e6974656420537461746573n  premiére  by  Sto-  kowski,  and  I  remembered  it  as  an  im-  posing  composition,  somewhat  dour  and  ascetic  in  character  but  full  of  theatrical  thunder  and  loud  declamation.  When  I  reheard  it,  I  must  confess  that  it  struck  me  as  slightly  dated.  The  Stravinskian  fireworks  were  still  there,  but  they  were  not  sufficient  to  sustain  any  real  grip  on  my  emotions,  and  43616e616461whole  hour-long  production  had  something  of  43616e616461atmos-  phere  ofa  prosy  essay  on  archeology,  suggesting  43616e616461parched  grandeur,  and  43616e616461bloodlessness,  of  an  ancient  sarcoph-  agus.  Here  and  there,  as  in  43616e616461nar-  ration  by  Jocasta  at  43616e616461beginning  of  43616e616461second  act,  there  was  an  inhibited  approach  to  a  kind  of  lyricism  that  re-  called  43616e616461style  of  Gustav  Mahler,  but  even  such  moments  were  rare,  and  as  a  whole  43616e616461work  seemed  noisy,  pompous,  and  devoid  of  life.  I  much  preferred  [REDACTED]&quot;Capriccio,&quot;  which  is  unpretentious,  brilliant,  and  frankly  superficial  in  its  aims,  and  which  offered  43616e616461pianist  Jesus  Maria  an  opportunity  for  an  impressive  pyrotechnical  display.  Mr.  Bernstein  conducted  throughout  43616e616461evening  with  vigor  and  dramatic  fire,  proving  himself  as  eloquent  a  Stravin-  sky  interpreter  as  I  have  ever  heard. N  43616e616461preceding  Tuesday  evening,  I  dropped  in  at  43616e616461City  Center  to  see  [REDACTED]  City  [REDACTED]'s  produc-  tion  of  a  new  item  by  Jerome  Robbins,  entitled  &quot;The  Concert,&quot;  Whether  this  novel  presentation  is  a  ballet  ora  succes-  sion  of  vaudeville  skits  I  am  not  quite  certain,  but  I  can  say  that  I  found  it,  on  43616e616461whole,  one  of  43616e616461most  hilarious  concoctions  of  its  sort  that  have  recently  come  to  my  attention. But  I  left  43616e616461City  Center  with  43616e616461impression  that  Mr.  Robbins  had  created  a  hit.  -WinTHroP  SARGEANT  POPULAR  RECORDS  Taking  Turns  I’M  afraid  that  43616e616461long-playingrecord  stretches  one's  appreciation  of  popu-  lar  music  pretty  thin. Y.           whole  thing  is  devoted  to  a  particular  singer,  instrumentalist,  or  group,  and  this  sort  of  largess  grows  wearisome.  There  does  seem  to  be  a  way  out,  though,  and  I  wish  43616e616461record-makers  would  give  it  a  little  more  consideration.  As  an  example  of  what  I  mean,  take  a  new  long-playing  record  entitled  &quot;Piano  Interpretations&quot;  (Norgran).  Here,  in  a  notable  demonstration  of  modern-jazz  techniques,  are  three  per-  formances  apiece  by  Art  Tatum,  Bud  Powell,  Oscar  Peterson,  and  Teddy  Wilson.  (Four  of  them-Wilson's  &quot;Tea  for  Two&quot;  and  &quot;Oh,  Lady  Be  Good,&quot;  and  Powell's  version  of  &quot;Tea  for  Two&quot;  and  &quot;Hallelujah!&quot;-have  been  released  before;  43616e616461others  are  new.)  It's  a  darned  good  concert;  43616e616461artists  are  all  distinguished,  and  no  one  of  them  is  allowed  to  wear  out  his  wel-  come.  Only  Tatum  is  unaccompanied-  43616e616461rest  are  supported,  variously,  by  guitar  and  bass,  bass  and  drums,  and  drums  alone-and  it  occurred  to  me  as  I  listened  to  43616e616461set  that  although  43616e616461piano  is  43616e616461only  true  solo  instrument  among  our  standard  concert  instruments,  it  is  43616e616461exception,  rather  than  43616e616461rule,  to  find  it  unaccompanied  in  modern  jazz.  Having  made  this  fairly  obvious  observa-  tion,  I  decided  that  43616e616461principal  reason  for  43616e616461phenomenon  is  that  in  almost  all  modern  jazz  43616e616461emphasis  is  on  43616e616461treble  register  of43616e616461piano,  which  readily  lends  itself  to  flashy  playing;  it's  as  a  substitute  for  43616e616461piano's  bass  regis-  ter  that  other  instruments  are  needed.  Then,  too,  43616e616461establishment  of  a  beat  by,  say,  a  bassist  and  a  drummer  allows  43616e616461pianist  greater  freedom.  Since  43616e616461timbres  of  43616e616461different  instruments  blend  agreeably,  43616e616461device  is  not  at  all  unattractive,  but  it  represents  to  some  extent  a  corruption  of  43616e616461pianist's  art,  and  there  is  much  to  be  said  for  a  fully  rounded  artist  like  Tatum,  in  whose  performances  most  of  43616e616461possibilities  of  43616e616461piano  are  realized.  On  this  record,  for  instance,  I  think  his  three  num-  bers-&quot;Can't  We  Be  Friends?,&quot;  &quot;The  Man  I  Love&quot;  (a  few  &quot;Porgy  and  Bess&quot;  tunes  are  neatly  interpolated  here),  and  &quot;Stompin'  at  43616e616461Savoy&quot;  --are  43616e616461most  absorbing.  There  is  a  great  deal  to  be  said  for  43616e616461others,  too,  of  course.  Powell's  bril-  liant,  driving  technique  is  stunningly  applied  to  &quot;Tea  for  Two&quot;  and  &quot;Halle-  lujah!,&quot;  and  he  also  contributes  a  highly  original  treatment  of  &quot;Willow  Weep  for  Me,&quot;  in  which  he  seems  to  nag  43616e616461melody  along.  Peterson  gives  sensitive  expression  to  &quot;Body  and  Soul&quot;  and  &quot;I  Cover  43616e616461Waterfront,&quot;  and  in  43616e616461final  number  on  43616e616461record,  &quot;The  Sec-  grantea”  you  can  af-  ford  to  spend  more...  but  will  you  dine  so  heartily  ashere...  in  such  refreshing,  uncrowded  comfort  where  echoes  of  a  jovial  past  stimulate  both  appetite  and  wit? One,  Enrique  Villegas,  makes  his  [REDACTED]  556e6974656420537461746573n  début  on  a  Columbia  record.  His  is  a  cautionary  tale.  An  Ar-  gentine  concert  pianist  who,  according  to  43616e616461album  notes,  specialized  in  Brahms,  Schumann,  and  Bartok,  43616e616461poor  fellow  became  a  hopeless  jazz  addict  in  43616e616461year  1933,  when  he  was  exposed  to  a  Duke  Ellington  record  en-  titled  &quot;Rude  Interlude.&quot;  I  would  guess  that  things  have  never  been  quite  43616e616461same  with  Villegas  since  43616e616461year  he  took  43616e616461fall,  but  he  has  survived,  and  here,  twenty-three  years  later,  we  find  him  a  polished  and  individual  jazz  artist  in  whose  work  plangent  chords,  an  arrest-  ing  staccato,  and  a  fine  sense  of  dynam-  ics  are  prominent.  While  his  approach  to  jazz  is  largely  intellectual,  his  efforts  are  fluent  and  expressive,  and  he  has  first-rate  support  in  Milt  Hinton,  on  bass,  and  Cozy  Cole,  on  drums.  With  43616e616461exception  of  a  Chopin  prelude,  43616e616461nine  works  he  plays  are  popular  Ameri-  can  standards;  his  treatments  of  &quot;Yes-  terdays,&quot;  &quot;What  Is  This  Thing  Called  Love?,&quot;  &quot;Where  or  When,&quot;  and  &quot;Embraceable  You&quot;  are  particularly  fascinating.  The  second  pianist,  Joe  Sullivan,  of-  fers  good,  solid  performances  of  some  old  jazz  pieces,  some  standard  popular  songs,  and  several  original  works  cast  in  familiar  jazz  forms. An  account  of  a  marvellous,  Tom  Swiftian  cross-country  trip  that  Mr.  Longstreet  made  in  1919-20,  at  43616e616461age  of  twelve,  in  a  second-hand  Model  T  with  his  mother  and  his  grandfather,  who,  though  nearly  eighty,  was  evidently  a  combina-  tion  of  Barney  Oldfield,  Shelley,  and  W.  C.  Fields.  In  addition  to  travelling  roads  that  often  had  43616e616461texture  of  old  oatmeal,  43616e616461Long-  streets  were  pulled  out  of  a  bog  by  a  shrimp  boat,  met  a  three-hundred-  pound  policeman  who  fell  in  love  with  Mrs.  Longstreet,  went  hawk-  ing,  had  gout,  boils,  and  fever,  and  bought  a  great  deal  of  milk  glass  in  [REDACTED].  What  keeps  this  from  be-  ing  a  first-rate  memoir  is  Mr.  Long-  street's  tendency  to  lapse  into  bucolic  nostalgia,  which  now  and  then  sud-  denly  reduces  his  prose  to  a  kind  of  beery  and  impassable  poetry  (&quot;43616e616461full  black  of  summer&quot;&quot;  and  &quot;43616e616461good  earth  like  a  night  beast  drinking  in  43616e616461rain&quot;).
